Chapter of Iddah. If a minor boy died leaving his wife and she was pregnant, her iddah is the delivery of her pregnancy. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: her iddah is four months and ten days, and this is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because the pregnancy's lineage from the boy is not established, so it became like a pregnancy occurring after death. For the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), there is the general application of Allah's saying (interpretation): 'And those with pregnancies, their term is that they give birth to their pregnancy.' And also because in women with pregnancy, the iddah is measured by the duration of the pregnancy, whether the period is short or long; it is not to know the emptiness of the womb, because the iddah of death is legislated with months despite the existence of menstrual periods, but rather to fulfill the right of marriage. And this meaning is realized in the minor boy even if the pregnancy is not from him, unlike a pregnancy that occurs after death, because iddah became obligatory by months and then does not change by the occurrence of pregnancy. In our case, as soon as it was obligatory, it was obligatory measured by the duration of the pregnancy, so they differ. And the wife of a major (adult) man is not affected if pregnancy occurs after death, because lineage is established from him, so it is as if the pregnancy existed at the time of death in legal effect. The lineage of the child is not established in either case because the minor has no sperm, so impregnation is not conceivable, and marriage stands in place of intercourse in the context of impression. Translation: And if a minor boy died leaving his wife, although she is pregnant, her iddah is that she delivers her pregnancy. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Imam Abu Yusuf said her iddah is four months and ten days, and this is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i. The reason is that the lineage of this pregnancy from the minor is not established, so it became as if the pregnancy occurred after the death. The proof of both parties (the two) is that Allah (تعالى) gave an absolute command that the iddah of pregnant women is to deliver their pregnancy. And a second proof is that in pregnant women, the iddah is measured by the period of delivery, whether short or long. And this is not to ascertain the emptiness of the womb, because the iddah of death is legislated with months despite the presence of menstrual periods, but rather to fulfill the right of marriage. And this meaning (fulfilling the right of marriage) is found in the minor child even if the pregnancy is not from him, unlike a pregnancy that occurs after death. The reason for the difference is that if iddah became obligatory by months, then the occurrence of pregnancy does not change it. In our case, when iddah was obligated, from that very moment it was obligated with a duration measured by the pregnancy, so the two cases are distinct. And the objection from the wife of a major man when pregnancy occurs after death does not apply, because lineage is established from him, so it is as if the pregnancy existed at the time of death in legal effect. The lineage of the child is not established in either case because the minor has no sperm, so impregnation is not conceivable, and marriage stands in place of intercourse in the context of impression. Explanation of the case: If a minor boy died leaving his pregnant wife, then according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), her iddah is delivery of the pregnancy. Imam Abu Yusuf said her iddah is four months and ten days. This is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that the lineage of this pregnancy from the minor is not established, so it became like a pregnancy appearing after the death of the minor. And in that case, by agreement, the iddah of death (four months ten days) is obligatory. Therefore, in the mentioned case, similarly four months ten days.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf) on the iddah of a minor's wife who is pregnant at his death. The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that her iddah ends with delivery, based on the Quranic verse (65:4) that applies to all pregnant women. Their reasoning is that the purpose of iddah in this context is to fulfill the right of marriage (haqq al-nikah), which exists even though the minor is incapable of producing a child; the pregnancy is incidental. They distinguish this from a pregnancy that occurs after the husband's death, where lineage is impossible and the iddah is fixed at four months ten days. Abu Yusuf and the Shafi'i school argue that since the child's lineage is not established from the minor (due to his incapacity), the pregnancy is treated as occurring after death, so the fixed iddah of iddat al-wafat applies. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the iddah is determined at the moment of death; if the woman is already pregnant, the iddah is measured by delivery, regardless of paternity. The passage also clarifies that for an adult man, if pregnancy occurs after death, the iddah remains four months ten days because lineage from the adult is established, making the pregnancy pre-existing in legal effect. The child's lineage from a minor is never established, but that does not change the ruling. This shows the intricate interplay between textual evidence, the purpose of iddah, and analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
وضع حمل (wad' haml): The delivery of a pregnancy; the moment a pregnant woman gives birth.
حق نکاح (haqq al-nikah): The right of marriage; the purpose of iddah in this context is to complete the rights of marriage, not merely to ascertain the emptiness of the womb.
ثبوت نسب (thubut nasab): The legal establishment of lineage of a child from the father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the iddah of a minor's pregnant wife?
A) Four months and ten days
B) Until she delivers the baby
C) Three months
D) No iddah because the marriage was not consummated

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband dies ends with delivery, even if the husband is a minor, due to the general application of the Quranic verse and the principle of haqq al-nikah.

Iddah of a Minor's Pregnant Wife
Intra-Hanafi debate on iddah of pregnant wife of a minor husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that the iddah of a minor husband's pregnant widow is by months and evaluate the counter-argument of Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A minor cannot father a child, so his widow's iddah is by months, not by delivery.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle case: when a minor husband dies and his wife is pregnant. Since the minor cannot be the father, the pregnancy is not legally attributed to him, and the Hanafi school rules that the iddah remains by months (four months and ten days). This contrasts with the case of an adult husband, where pregnancy changes the iddah to delivery. The text presents the reasoning of both sides and settles on the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the iddah of a pregnant widow of a minor husband is four months and ten days (by months), not by delivery.
This is the mufta bihi position, confirmed by the author of Quduri.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that her iddah ends with delivery, analogizing to the case of an adult husband.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the opposing school here is within the Hanafi school.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Abu Yusuf relies on the generality of Quran 65:4 ('And those who are pregnant – their term is when they give birth') and the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' (the child belongs to the bed), arguing that marriage alone establishes paternity.
The key point is the application of the hadith to a minor.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the purpose of iddah is to fulfill the right of marriage, not merely to clear the womb. For a minor, intercourse is impossible, so lineage is not proven. The analogy to an adult is invalid because in the adult case paternity is established; for the minor, it is not. The hadith applies only where intercourse is possible.
The distinction hinges on the possibility of intercourse and establishment of lineage.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: If a minor husband dies and his wife is pregnant, according to Abu Yusuf her iddah ends with delivery; according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, her iddah is four months and ten days from death, regardless of delivery.
The mufta bihi is the latter view.

Chapter of Iddah. Completing (the iddah) is necessary. 129. Ashraf al-Bidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya Volume 5. The argument of both sides is that Allah Most High commanded absolutely that the iddah of pregnant women is delivery (of the child). Whether that iddah is of divorce or of death, and whether the pregnancy is from the husband or from someone else. So this verse, due to its generality, includes the wife of this minor child, because she too is pregnant, even though her pregnancy is not proven in lineage. And the second proof is that for pregnant women, the iddah of death is estimated with the period of delivery, whether this period is short or long. And the purpose of this iddah is to fulfill the right of marriage, not to discover the emptiness of the womb from pregnancy. The reason is that iddah of death, in the presence of menstruation, is prescribed with months. So it is proven that the purpose of this iddah is to fulfill the right of marriage, not to ascertain the emptiness of the womb. And this meaning, i.e., fulfilling the right of marriage, is also found in the (case of the) child, even if (the child's) action does not establish (lineage). This is in contrast to the answer to Imam Abu Yusuf's analogy regarding the pregnancy that occurs after death. The summary of the answer is that it is not correct to analogize the (pregnancy) existing at the time of death to the one occurring after death, because if there is no pregnancy at the time of death, iddah with months is initially obligated so that the right of marriage may be fulfilled. So now if pregnancy appears later, the iddah will not change due to the occurrence of pregnancy. And in this issue, the iddah is initially estimated with the period of pregnancy, because the iddah of pregnant women is that they deliver their pregnancy. Thus, the distinction between the analogized (case) and the analogized-upon (case) has become clear. For this reason, analogizing one to the other is not correct. However, an objection arises: why does the iddah change in the case of an adult man's wife? That is, if an adult man (dies) and his wife's pregnancy is not apparent at that time, so iddah by months becomes necessary, then it becomes apparent that she is pregnant, then her iddah changes and is set to delivery. It is obligatory in this case that lineage is proven from the adult man. So this pregnancy became like one existing at the time of death. The author of Quduri states that from a minor, the child's lineage is not proven in both cases, whether the child is present at the time of death or born later, because a minor does not have sperm. Therefore, pregnancy from him is not conceivable. But if someone says that marriage exists, so due to the Prophet's (peace be upon him) saying 'the child belongs to the bed,' marriage should be made a substitute for intercourse, then the answer is that marriage is a substitute for intercourse only when intercourse is possible from it, and here intercourse is not conceivable from a minor. Therefore, marriage cannot be a substitute for intercourse.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the iddah of a pregnant wife of a minor husband. The discussion revolves around whether the wife's iddah is determined by delivery (as per the general verse of the Quran) or by months (since the pregnancy is not attributed to the minor). The text presents two opposing proofs: one side argues that the verse about pregnant women's iddah is general and applies even if the pregnancy is not from the husband, but the other side argues that the purpose of iddah is to fulfill the right of marriage, not just to clear the womb. The author then addresses an objection regarding adult men, explaining that if a pregnancy appears after the husband's death, the iddah changes to delivery because lineage is proven. However, for a minor, lineage is not proven because a minor cannot father a child, so the iddah remains by months. The author of Quduri confirms that a minor's child is not attributed to him in any case, and the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' does not apply because intercourse is not possible from a minor.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
نابالغ (nābaligh): A minor who has not reached puberty; legally incapable of fathering a child.
الولد للفراش (al-waladu li al-firāsh): A hadith meaning the child belongs to the (marital) bed; used to establish paternity in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the iddah of a minor husband's pregnant widow?
A) She observes iddah by delivery because the Quranic verse is general.
B) She observes iddah by months because the minor cannot be the father.
C) She observes no iddah because the marriage is invalid.
D) She observes iddah by three menstrual cycles.

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a minor husband's pregnant wife is determined by months (four months and ten days), not by delivery, because the pregnancy is not attributed to him.

The Menstrual Period of Divorce Is Excluded from 'Iddah
Ruling excluding the ongoing menstrual period from iddah count

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the menstrual period in which divorce occurs is not counted toward the 'iddah and explain the reason based on the requirement of complete cycles.

Core Idea
The menstrual period of divorce is excluded because 'iddah requires three complete cycles.

This lesson explains a precise rule in Hanafi family law: when a husband divorces his wife during her menstruation, the ongoing cycle does not count as one of the three cycles required for the 'iddah. This rule prevents errors in calculating the waiting period and ensures the 'iddah is observed fully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and context
The ruling applies when a man divorces his wife while she is menstruating. This is the scenario given in the text.
The divorce itself is valid even though it occurs during menstruation (considered bid'ah divorce).
2
State the ruling clearly
The text states: she does not count that menstrual period (haydah) toward her 'iddah. In other words, the 'iddah begins after that period ends.
The Arabic phrase 'lam ta'tad' means she does not consider it as part of the 'iddah.
3
Understand the reasoning
The 'iddah is prescribed as three complete menstrual cycles (kawāmil). A partial cycle in which divorce occurs is not complete and cannot be counted.
The principle is preservation of the prescribed number; reduction is not allowed.
4
Apply to a concrete example
If a woman is divorced on the first day of her menstruation, she completes that period, then waits for three full cycles after it. The initial period is excluded.
This ensures exactly three full cycles are observed.

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling of the Hanafi school: if a man divorces his wife while she is menstruating, the menstrual period in which the divorce took place is not counted as one of the three menstrual cycles required for the iddah. The reason is that the iddah is prescribed as three complete menstrual cycles, and this partial period does not count as a full cycle. The ruling is based on the principle that the iddah cannot be reduced. The text includes the original Arabic matn and its Urdu translation and commentary. This ruling applies to divorces during menstruation, which is considered a bid'ah (innovative) divorce but still valid.

Key Terms
حيضة (haydah): A single menstrual period (the time during which a woman menstruates).
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death, fixed by Shariah.

Interpenetration of Two Iddahs (Tadakhul)
Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on tadakhul of iddahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tadakhul of two iddahs and explain the key difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Two iddahs interpenetrate because the goal is clearing the womb, not a separate act of worship.

When a woman already in iddah is subjected to doubtful intercourse, a second iddah becomes obligatory. The Hanafi school holds that these two iddahs interpenetrate (tadakhul), meaning the same menstrual periods count for both. The Shafi'i school disagrees, viewing iddah primarily as worship. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that a woman in iddah from divorce or death has intercourse under a mistaken belief of permissibility (shubhah). She now owes two iddahs.
The second iddah is from doubtful intercourse, not a valid marriage.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the Hanafi school, the two iddahs interpenetrate. The woman counts her menstrual periods for both simultaneously. If the first iddah ends before the second, she must complete the second.
Tadakhul means the periods serve both obligations.
3
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the two iddahs do not interpenetrate. Each must be observed separately, as iddah is an act of worship analogous to fasting.
Like two fasts cannot be kept on one day, two iddahs cannot run concurrently.
4
Compare the proofs
Hanafi proof: The primary purpose of iddah is to ascertain the emptiness of the womb, achieved by one set of periods. Worship is subsidiary. Shafi'i proof: Iddah is worship (refraining), so it cannot be combined.
Hanafi cites that iddah ends without the woman's knowledge, showing worship is not the primary goal.
5
Note the practical consequence
Under Hanafi, the woman can remarry after the longer iddah ends. Under Shafi'i, she must complete two separate iddahs sequentially, delaying remarriage.
The difference affects the woman's waiting period and ability to remarry.

Source Text

And when a woman in iddah is subjected to doubtful intercourse, she must observe another iddah, and the two iddahs interpenetrate, and whatever menstruation the woman sees will be counted from both of them. And when the first iddah ends while the second is not complete, she must complete the second iddah. This is according to us. Imam al-Shafi'i said: They do not interpenetrate, because the purpose is worship – it is worship of refraining from marriage and going out, so they do not interpenetrate just as two fasts on one day do not interpenetrate. Our proof is that the purpose is to ascertain the emptiness of the womb, and that is achieved by one (iddah), so they interpenetrate. The meaning of worship is subsidiary. Do you not see that iddah ends without her knowledge and despite her leaving off the refraining? Explanation of the issue: If two iddahs become obligatory upon a woman, there are two scenarios: they may be from two men or from one man. If from one man, the scenario is that the husband gave three divorces, then thinking it was permissible, the same husband married her during iddah and had intercourse, or had intercourse with a thrice-divorced woman thinking it permissible, or had intercourse with a bainah divorcée during iddah. In this case, by consensus the two iddahs interpenetrate. If the two iddahs are from two men, then there are two scenarios: the two iddahs may be of the same type or different. If they are of different types, e.g., a widow subjected to doubtful intercourse, the ruling is mentioned in the following issue. If they are of the same type, e.g., a divorcée married another man during her iddah and he had intercourse with her, and then they were separated, then according to us the two iddahs interpenetrate. And whatever menstruation she sees will be counted in both iddahs. And if the first iddah ends while the second is not complete, she must complete the second iddah.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi ruling on what happens when a woman who is already in an iddah (due to divorce or death) engages in doubtful intercourse (shubhah) with another man. The Hanafi position is that the two iddahs interpenetrate (tadakhul). This means that the woman counts her menstrual periods as fulfilling both iddahs simultaneously. However, if the first iddah ends before the second is complete, she must finish the second iddah. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that iddah is an act of worship ('ibadah) – refraining from marriage and going out – so two separate iddahs cannot be combined, just as one cannot combine two fasts on the same day. The Hanafi response is that the primary purpose of iddah is to ascertain that the womb is empty (faraqh al-rahim), which is achieved by one iddah. The worship aspect is subsidiary, as seen in cases where the iddah ends without the woman's knowledge or even if she fails to refrain. The text then explains various scenarios: if the two iddahs arise from the same man (e.g., he divorces her three times and then has intercourse during iddah), there is consensus on tadakhul. If from two men, the ruling depends on whether the iddahs are of the same type (both from divorce or both from death) or different. The passage is cut off before completing the discussion of different types.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Interpenetration of two legal obligations such that fulfilling one fulfills the other.
شبهة (shubhah): Doubtful intercourse – sexual relations that a person mistakenly believes to be lawful but legally are not, or where the permissibility is uncertain.
فراغ رحم (farāgh al-rahim): Emptiness of the womb – the purpose of iddah is to ensure no pregnancy exists from the previous marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the woman cannot distinguish between the two periods.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, two iddahs interpenetrate because the primary objective is to ascertain emptiness of the womb, not separate acts of worship.

intermediate

Beginning of 'Iddah: Standard Rule and Fatwa on Confession
Ruling with proof on 'iddah commencement and a practical fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the beginning of 'iddah in divorce and death and explain the fatwa of the mashayikh regarding iqrar, citing the reason of avoiding collusion.

Core Idea
The 'iddah begins immediately upon divorce or death, but Hanafi fatwa starts it from the husband's confession to prevent collusion.

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a foundational rule: the 'iddah waiting period begins right when divorce or death occurs, not when the woman learns of it. However, the mashayikh of Bukhara and Samarkand issued a fatwa for divorce cases: to prevent spouses from conspiring to falsely claim an expired 'iddah, the period is calculated from the husband's confession (iqrar). This lesson contrasts the original rule with the practical fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is the 'iddah (waiting period) for a woman after divorce or death of her husband. The ruling is that 'iddah is wajib (obligatory) for a free woman who has been consummated.
The hukm level is wajib, not fard, because the evidence is zanni al-thubut (from ahad hadith).
2
State the standard rule for beginning
The standard rule: 'iddah begins immediately at the time of divorce (talaq) or death (mawt). This is the position of all four Sunni schools.
Knowledge of the event is not a condition.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is cause-effect reasoning: the obligation of 'iddah (effect) follows its cause (talaq/mawt) without delay. The cause is the divorce or death itself, not the woman's awareness.
Some scholars note that the real cause is the marriage contract, and divorce is a condition, but the immediate connection holds.
4
Recognize the fatwa exception
The mashayikh of Bukhara and Samarkand gave a fatwa in divorce cases: if the husband confesses to a past divorce, the 'iddah begins from the time of confession (iqrar). This is to avoid the suspicion of collusion (tuhmat al-muwada'ah).
The fatwa applies only when the husband claims a past divorce and the wife confirms; it is not the general rule.
5
Compare positions and identify mufta bihi
The standard position is from zahir al-riwayah, but the fatwa of the mashayikh is the operative ruling (mufta bihi) in cases where collusion is suspected. The tarjih is based on practical necessity to prevent fraud.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

طلاق یا وفات کا علم نہیں ہوا یہاں تک کہ مدت عدت گزر گئی تو اس کی عدت پوری ہوچیکی کیونکہ عدت واجب ہونے کا سبب (یہی) طلاق یا وفات ہے تو سب پائے جانے کی وقت سے عدت کی ابتداء معتبر ہوگی۔ اور ہمارے مشائخ طلاق کی صورت میں یہ فتویٰ دیتے تھے کہ عدت کی ابتداء اقراء کے وقت سے ہوگی تاکہ باہمی اتفاق کر لینے کی تہمت دور ہو ۔ تشریح مصنف نے فرمایا کہ طلاق کی صورت میں عدت کی ابتداء طلاق دینے کے بعد سے ہوگی اور وفات کی صورت میں شوہر کی وفات کے بعد سے عدت کی ابتداء ہوگی چنانچہ اگر شوہر نے طلاق دی اور عورت کو اس کا علم نہیں ہو سکایا شوہر کی وفات ہوگئی اور عورت بے خبر رہی یہاں تک عدت کا زمانہ گذر گیا تو حکم یہ ہے کہ اس کی عدت پوری ہو چکی معلوم ہونے کے بعد عدت کا اعادہ نہیں ہوگا۔ ائمہ اربعہ کا یہی مذہب ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے عدت کو واجب کرنے والی علت طلاق یا وفات ہے پس وجوب عدت معلول ہوا اور طلاق یا وفات علت ہوئی اور معلول اپنی علت کے ساتھ متصل ہوتا ہے اس لئے وجوب عدت اپنی علت یعنی طلاق یا وفات کے ساتھ متصل ہوگا اور علت اور سبب پائے جانے کے وقت سے ہی عدت کی ابتداء معتبر ہوگی فتح القدیر میں لکھا ہے وجوب عدت کا سبب طلاق کو قرار دینا تساہل ہے کیونکہ عدت کا سبب وہ نکاح ہے جو دخول کیساتھ متاکہ ہو اور طلاق وجوب عدت کے لئے شرط ہے۔ مناسب یہ تھا کہ صاحب ہدایہ یوں فرماتے لان عند الطلاق والموت يتم السبب فيتعقبها من غير فصل یعنی طلاق اور موت کے وقت سبب پورا ہو جاتا ہے لہذا عدت اس کے فورا بعد بغیر کسی فصل کے واجب ہوگی۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ بخار اسمر قند کے علماء کا طلاق کی صورت میں فتویٰ یہ تھا کہ عدت کی ابتداء اقرأ کے وقت سے ہوگی مثلا مرد اپنی بیوی سے ایک زمانہ جدا ر ہا پھر اس نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا کہ میں تجھے اتنے روز پہلے طلاق دے چکا اور عورت کو اس کا علم نہیں پس اگر عورت نے اس کی تصدیق کی تو اس کی عدت کی ابتداء اسی وقت سے ہوگی۔ اور دلیل اس پر یہ ہے باہمی اتفاق کر لینے کی تہمت دور ہو سکے یعنی ممکن ہے کہ میاں بیوی نے طلاق اور انقضاء عدت پر اتفاق کر لیا ہوتا کہ شوہر ا سکے لئے دین کا اقرار کرے یا وصیت کر دے پس اس تہمت کو دور کرنے کے لئے اقرار کے وقت سے اس کی عدت کا انتہار کیا جائے گا۔
And the beginning of 'iddah in divorce is immediately after divorce, and in death immediately after death. If she does not know of the divorce or death until the period of 'iddah has passed, then her 'iddah has expired because the cause of the obligation of 'iddah is divorce or death, so its beginning is considered from the time of the existence of the cause. And our mashayikh (scholars of Bukhara and Samarkand) give fatwa in divorce that its beginning is from the time of confession (iqrar) to avoid the accusation of collusion.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the beginning of 'iddah. The author states that for divorce, the 'iddah begins immediately after the divorce is pronounced, and for death, immediately after the husband's death. If the woman is unaware of the divorce or death and the entire 'iddah period passes, her 'iddah is considered complete because the cause of 'iddah is the divorce or death itself, not her knowledge. This is the position of all four Sunni schools. The proof given is that the obligation of 'iddah (the effect) follows its cause (divorce or death) without delay, as the effect is connected to its cause. A note from Fath al-Qadir points out that it is technically the marriage contract that is the cause, and divorce is a condition, but the meaning is the same. However, the author reports that the Hanafi mashayikh of Bukhara and Samarkand gave a fatwa to avoid collusion between spouses: in divorce cases, the 'iddah begins from the time the husband confesses (iqrar) to the divorce, not from the actual date. For example, if the husband says, 'I divorced you many days ago,' and the wife confirms, then the 'iddah is calculated from the time of this confession. This is to prevent the couple from conspiring to falsely claim that the 'iddah has already expired to deprive the wife of her rights or inheritance.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period prescribed for a woman after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
اقرار (iqrar): In this context, the husband's admission or confession that he has divorced his wife in the past.
تہمت مواضعہ (tuhmat al-muwada'ah): Suspicion of collusion between spouses to agree upon a false divorce date to cheat the wife's rights or inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the standard Hanafi ruling, when does the iddah begin for a woman who is divorced without her knowledge?
A) From the time of her knowledge
B) From the time of divorce
C) From the time of confession
D) From the time of separation

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah begins at the time of divorce or death, not when the woman learns, but in divorce cases, the fatwa starts it from the husband's confession to prevent collusion.

Beginning of 'Iddah in Irregular Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the start of 'iddah in fasid marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Zufar's positions on the start of 'iddah in a fasid marriage and state which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
In a fasid marriage, the 'iddah begins only when further intercourse is impossible — separation or firm resolve.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when does the 'iddah (waiting period) begin in an irregular (fasid) marriage? The majority Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) argue that it begins after judicial separation or the husband's firm resolve to cease intercourse, while Imam Zufar holds it begins from the last actual intercourse. We will analyze the reasoning behind each position and see why the majority view is relied upon.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the core legal question: When does the 'iddah start in a fasid marriage?
2
Identify the two positions
Contrast the Hanafi majority view (after separation/resolve) with Zufar's view (from last intercourse).
3
Examine the proofs
For each position, cite the evidence: Zufar relies on intercourse as cause; Hanafis argue all intercourses under one contract are treated as one, and the last is only determined when further intercourse is impossible.
4
Understand the reasoning
Dig deeper: The Hanafi position uses the principle that ability in a state of shubha stands in place of actual intercourse, delaying the start of 'iddah until that ability is removed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the Hanafi majority view is relied upon in fatwa as it is the view of the majority and found in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعدة في النكاح الفاسد عقيب التفريق او عزم الواطى على ترك وطيها وقال زفر من اخر الوطيات لان الوطى هو السبب الموجب ولنا ان كل وطى وجد في العقد الفاسد يجرى مجرى الوطية الواحدة لاستناد الكل الى حكم عقد واحد ولهذا يكتفى فى الكل بمهر واحد فقبل المتاركة او العزم لا تثبت العدة مع جواز وجود غيره ولان التمكن على وجه الشبهة اقيم مقام حقيقة الوطى لخفائه ومسائل الحاجة الى معرفة الحكم في حق غيره تشریح مصنف نے فرمایا کہ نکاح فاسد کی صورت میں عدت کی ابتداء اس وقت سے ہوگی جبکہ حاکم زوجین کے درمیان تفریق کر دے یا واطی ترک وطی کا عزم محکم کرے اور امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ نکاح فاسد میں عدت کی ابتداء آخری وطی سے ہوگی ۔ امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وطی ہی عدت کے واجب ہونے کا سبب ہے اس لئے کہ اگر یہ شخص وطی نہ کرتا تو اس عورت پر عدت واجب نہ ہوتی۔ اس وجہ سے ہم نے کہا کہ آخری وطی سے عدت کا زمانہ شروع ہو جائے گا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ ہمیں تسلیم ہے کہ وطی ہی عدت واجب ہونے کا سبب ہے مگر جتنی وطیات عقد فاسد میں پائی گئیں وہ سب ایک وطی کے مرتبہ میں ہیں، کیونکہ تمام وطیات ایک عقد کی طرف منسوب ہیں۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ تمام وطیات کی وجہ سے ایک مہر واجب ہوتا ہے۔ پس آخری وطی جس پر عدت کا ترتب ہو وہ تفریق کر دینے سے ثابت ہوگی یا ترک وطی کا عزم کر لینے سے کیونکہ اس سے پہلے عدت ثابت نہیں ہوگی اس لئے کہ ابھی اس کے غیر یعنی وطی کا پایا جانا ممکن نہیں ہے۔ دلیل ثانی کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حقیقی وطی ایسا امر خفی ہے جس کا سبب ظاہر یعنی شبہ کے طور پر قدرت علی الوطی موجود ہے اور ہر وہ امر خفی جس کے لئے سبب ظاہر ہو تو وہ سبب ظاہر اس امر خفی کا قائم مقام اور مدار حکم ہوتا ہے جیسے شبہ کے طور پر قدرت علی الوطی حقیقی وطی کے قائم مقام ہے۔ پس جب تک قدرت علی الوطی باقی ہے تو گویا وطی باقی ہے لہذا اس پر قدرت رہتے ہوئے آخری وطی متعین نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ ہر وطی کے بعد قدرت علی الوطی باقی رہتی ہے، اس وجہ سے ہم نے کہا کہ دونوں کے درمیان تفریق یا ترک وطی کا عزم بالجزم ضروری ہے تا کہ قدرت علی الوطی مرتفع ہو جائے اور آخری وطی متعین ہو جائے۔
And the waiting period in irregular marriage is after separation or the man's firm resolve to stop intercourse. Zufar said: from the last intercourse because intercourse is the cause that necessitates it. Our evidence: every intercourse that occurs in the irregular marriage runs in the status of a single intercourse because all are attributed to one contract, and therefore one dowry suffices for all. So before separation or resolve, the waiting period is not established given the possibility of another, and because ability in the manner of shubha is placed in the position of actual intercourse due to its concealed nature, and the need to know the ruling for others. Commentary: The author says that in irregular marriage, the beginning of the waiting period is when the judge separates them or the man firmly resolves to abandon intercourse. Imam Zufar said: the beginning is from the last intercourse. Zufar's evidence: intercourse is the cause of obligation, for if he did not have intercourse, the woman would not have to observe waiting period. Therefore we say the period begins from the last. Our evidence: we concede intercourse is the cause, but all acts in irregular marriage are like one intercourse because they relate to one contract, hence one dowry. So the last intercourse is determined by separation or resolve, as before that the possibility of another exists. Second evidence: actual intercourse is hidden, but the apparent cause (ability in shubha) stands in its place, and as long as ability remains, the last intercourse is not determined. Therefore separation or firm resolve is needed to remove ability and determine the last intercourse.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the commencement of the 'iddah (waiting period) in a fasid (irregular) marriage. The relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that the 'iddah begins from the time of judicial separation or from the man's firm resolve to cease intercourse. Imam Zufar, a prominent Hanafi jurist, argues it should begin from the last act of intercourse, since intercourse is the cause of the 'iddah. The Hanafis counter that all intercourses under a single fasid contract are treated as one, as evidenced by the single dowry requirement. Therefore, only when the possibility of further intercourse is eliminated (by separation or resolve) does the last intercourse become determinable. They further argue that the ability to have intercourse in a state of shubha (doubt) substitutes for actual intercourse due to its hidden nature. Hence, the 'iddah cannot begin while that ability persists. The discussion is based on Qiyas (analogy) and Istihsan (juristic preference).

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage that is irregular due to a defect (e.g., missing witness) but not invalid (batil).
شبهة (shubha): A state of doubt or semblance that can give some legal effect to an otherwise invalid act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the 'iddah begin in a fasid marriage?
A) From the first intercourse
B) From the last intercourse
C) After judicial separation or the man's firm resolve to abandon intercourse
D) From the date of the marriage contract

Key Takeaway
In a fasid marriage, the 'iddah begins only when the possibility of further intercourse is eliminated — either by judicial separation or by the husband's firm resolve.

Woman's Claim of 'Iddah Completion
Ruling on credibility of woman's statement about her iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman's claim of iddah completion and identify the qiyas that supports it.

Core Idea
The woman's word about her iddah is accepted with an oath because she is like a trustee (ameen) in a matter only she can know.

In this lesson, we examine a procedural rule from Hanafi fiqh: when a woman claims her iddah has ended, her statement is accepted but she must swear an oath. This is because she has exclusive knowledge of her menstrual cycles, making her akin to a depositary. However, to prevent false claims, an oath is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The case: a woman in iddah says her waiting period is over, but her husband denies it. What is the ruling?
2
State the Hanafi position
Her statement is accepted along with her oath.
This is because she is considered ameen (trustworthy).
3
Identify the basis (illah)
She has exclusive knowledge of her own cycles; only she can know when iddah ends.
4
Examine the qiyas
She is analogized to a depositary (muda') who claims the deposit is returned or lost – his word is accepted with oath.
The shared illah is exclusive knowledge plus the potential for falsehood.
5
Note the oath requirement
Because she might be dishonest, an oath is necessary to confirm her statement.
Without the oath, her word would not be accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قالت المعتدة انقضت عدتى وكذبها الزوج كان القول قولها مع اليمين لانها امينة في ذالك وقد اتهمت بالكذب فتحلف كالمودع تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ معتدہ عورت نے کہا کہ میری عدت پوری ہوگئی اور اس کے شوہر نے اس کی تکذیب کی اور عدت گذرنے کے انکار کیا تو اس صورت میں عورت کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ عورت عدت گزرنے کی خبر دینے میں امین ہے کیونکہ اس کا علم سوائے عورت کے کسی دوسرے کو نہیں ہو سکتا مگر چونکہ عورت کے جھوٹ بولنے کا بھی احتمال ہے اس لئے عورت سے قسم لی جائے گی جیسے وہ شخص جس کے پاس امانت رکھی ہوئی تھی اس نے دعوی کیا کہ میں امانت واپس کر چکا یا ہلاک ہونے کا دعوی کیا اور جس کی امانت تھی اس نے اس کی تکذیب کی تو اس صورت میں مودع سے قسم لی جائے گی اور مع الیمین اس کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔
And if the woman observing 'iddah says, 'My 'iddah has ended', and her husband denies it, then her statement is accepted along with her oath, because she is trustworthy in that matter, and she is accused of lying, so she takes an oath like a depositary. Commentary: The issue is that a woman in 'iddah said her 'iddah is complete, and her husband denied and rejected that it had passed; in this case the woman's word with an oath is credible. The evidence is that the woman is trustworthy regarding the end of 'iddah because only she can know it. However, since she might lie, she is required to take an oath, like the case of a trustee who claims to have returned the deposit or that it perished, and the owner denies; then the trustee's statement with an oath is accepted.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof. The author states that when a woman in 'iddah claims her waiting period has ended, and her husband denies it, her statement is accepted but she must also swear an oath. The reason is that knowledge of menstruation and purity cycles is specific to her, making her akin to a trustee (ameen). However, due to potential dishonesty, an oath is required to corroborate her claim. This is analogous to the case of a depositary (mustawda') who claims to have returned the deposit or its destruction; his word is accepted with an oath. The ruling applies only to matters where the woman has exclusive knowledge. The Hanafi position relies on istihsan and consideration of custom.

Key Terms
المعتدة (al-mu'taddah): The woman who is observing the 'iddah (waiting period) after divorce or death.
امين (ameen): A trustworthy person whose word is accepted in matters known only to them.
المودع (al-muda'): The person with whom a deposit is placed (mustawda').

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because she is always truthful
B) Because she has exclusive knowledge and is like a trustee
C) Because the husband has no right to question her
D) Because the court compels the husband to accept

Key Takeaway
The woman's claim of iddah completion is accepted with an oath because she is a trustee in a matter only she can know.

Mahr and 'Iddah When Remarrying in 'Iddah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr and 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar on mahr and 'iddah in the scenario of remarrying during 'iddah and divorcing before consummation, and explain the Hanafi majority's proof based on constructive possession.

Core Idea
Remarrying in 'iddah merges possession—so the second divorce is after consummation, giving full mahr and a fresh 'iddah.

When a man divorces his wife bain (irrevocably) and then remarries her during her 'iddah, the legal consequences of a subsequent divorce before consummation become contested. The Hanafi school has three distinct views. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of each imam and explains why Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's position—that full mahr and a fresh 'iddah are due—is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the husband owes full mahr and the wife must observe a fresh, independent 'iddah. This treats the second divorce as if it occurred after consummation.
The key is that the wife is considered 'in possession' (maqbuda) due to the first intercourse and remaining 'iddah.
2
Identify the opposing internal views
Imam Muhammad argues that only half mahr is due and she completes the first 'iddah, because the second divorce happened before actual consummation. Imam Zufar argues she has no 'iddah at all—the first 'iddah ended with remarriage and the second never began.
Muhammad sees only the apparent act; Zufar sees a termination of the first 'iddah.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Muhammad's proof: The second divorce is before consummation (no new intercourse), so only half mahr applies, and the first 'iddah merely resumes. Zufar's proof: Remarriage aborts the first 'iddah; the second divorce happens before any new tie, so no 'iddah is due.
Both rely on the literal sequence of events.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the wife is still in the husband's constructive possession (qabd hukmi) from the first marriage because the 'iddah is a remnant of that marriage. Remarriage while she is in this state makes the possession actual, so the second divorce is de facto after consummation.
They analogize to a usurper who buys the usurped property—the contract alone transfers possession.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome differs significantly: under the majority view, the woman gets full mahr and must wait a fresh 'iddah; under Muhammad's view, half mahr and a shortened 'iddah; under Zufar's view, no 'iddah at all.
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

واذا طلق الرجل امرأته طلاق بائنا ثم تزوجها في عدتها وطلقها قبل الدخول بها فعليه مهر كامل وعليها عدة مستقلة وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وابى يوسف وقال محمد عليه نصف المهر وعليها اتمام العدة الأولى لان هذا طلاق قبل الميسس فلا يوجب كمال المهر ولا استيتاف العدة واكمال العدة الاولى انما يجب بالطلاق الاول الا انه لم يظهر حال التزوج الثانى فاذا ارتفع بالطلاق الثانى ظهر حكمه كما لو اشترى ام ولد ثم اعتقها ولهما انها مقبوضة في يده حقيقة بالوطية الأولى وبقى اثره وهو العدة فاذا جدد النكاح وهى مقبوضة ناب ذالك القبض عن القبض المستحق فى هذا النكاح كالغاصب يشترى المغصوب الذي في يده يصير قابضا بمجرد العقد فوضح بهذا انه طلاق بعد الدخول وقال زفر لا عدة عليها اصلالان الأولى قد سقطت بالتزوج فلا تعود والثانية لم تجب وجوابه ماقلنا
And if a man divorces his wife with a bain divorce, then marries her during her 'iddah, and divorces her before consummation, then the full mahr is obligatory upon him and a fresh 'iddah upon her. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: half the mahr is upon him, and she must complete the first 'iddah, because this is a divorce before consummation and does not require full mahr nor a fresh 'iddah; the completion of the first 'iddah is only obligatory due to the first divorce, but it did not manifest during the second marriage; when it is removed by the second divorce, its ruling appears, like one who buys an umm walad then frees her. And their (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's) evidence: she was actually in his possession by the first intercourse and its effect—the 'iddah—remained; so when he renewed the marriage while she was in his possession, that possession substitutes for the possession due in this marriage, like a usurper who buys the usurped property in his hand becomes possessor by mere contract. Thus it is clear that this is a divorce after consummation. Zufar said: no 'iddah upon her at all, because the first 'iddah fell by remarriage and does not return, and the second was not established. Our response is what we said.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute in a specific scenario: A man divorces his wife with a bain (irrevocable) divorce. He then remarries her during her 'iddah (while she is still in waiting). Before consummating the second marriage, he divorces her again. The question: What mahr is due, and what 'iddah must she observe? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the full mahr is due and she must observe a fresh, independent 'iddah (from the second divorce). Imam Muhammad rules that only half the mahr is due and she must complete the 'iddah from the first divorce, since the second divorce occurred before consummation, and the first 'iddah merely resumed. Zufar rules that she has no 'iddah at all because the first 'iddah was terminated by the remarriage and the second never started. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue that the wife was effectively in the husband's possession due to the first intercourse and ongoing 'iddah; the second marriage merged with that possession, so the second divorce is treated as after consummation, hence full mahr and fresh 'iddah. They draw an analogy to a usurper who buys the usurped property in his hand—the contract itself grants possession. The passage concludes with the Hanafi majority view being the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately, but still requires 'iddah.
مهر (mahr): The mandatory bridal gift (dower) from husband to wife; half if divorce before consummation, full if after.
قبض (qabd): Possession or physical control; in this context, legal possession via 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of early Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling on mahr when a man remarries his divorced wife during her 'iddah and divorces her before consummation?
A) No mahr is due
B) Half the mahr of the second marriage is due
C) Full mahr of the second marriage is due
D) The mahr of the first marriage is reinstated

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a man remarries his wife during her 'iddah and then divorces her without consummation, the majority view holds that full mahr and a fresh 'iddah are due because the 'iddah constitutes constructive possession.

Woman's Claim of 'Iddah Completion Accepted with Oath
Ruling on accepting woman's claim of 'iddah completion with oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a woman's claim of 'iddah completion and explain the qiyas analogy to a depositary.

Core Idea
A woman's claim of 'iddah ending is accepted with an oath, like a depositary's claim.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a woman claims her waiting period ('iddah) has ended, but her husband denies it. How does Hanafi fiqh resolve this? The ruling is that the woman's word is accepted, but she must take an oath. The proof comes from a qiyas (analogy) to the law of deposits. Let's unpack the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage rules that the woman's statement (qawl) is accepted, but with an oath (yamin). Note the condition: the husband must deny her claim. If he does not deny, no oath is needed.
The ruling applies only when the husband disputes the end of the 'iddah.
2
Locate the legal proof
The proof is not a direct text from Quran or Sunnah, but an analogy (qiyas). The woman is compared to a depositary (muda').
The illa (effective cause) is trustworthiness combined with potential dishonesty.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah of the qiyas
Identify the original case (asl): a depositary claims to have returned or destroyed the deposit. His word is accepted with an oath because he is the only one who knows the truth, yet he may lie. The same illa applies to the woman: she alone knows her 'iddah, and she may lie. Therefore, her word is accepted with an oath.
This is a clear qiyas jali (evident analogy).
4
Check for ikhtilaf
The passage indicates no intra-Hanafi disagreement. The ruling is consistent across the school and accepted as mufta bihi.
There is no opposing position mentioned.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the woman's statement with an oath is accepted. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

And if the woman undergoing 'iddah says, 'My 'iddah has ended,' and the husband denies her, the statement is hers with an oath, because she is trustworthy in that matter, and she is accused of lying, so she takes an oath like the depositary. Translation: If the woman says her 'iddah has passed and the husband denies, the woman's statement is accepted with an oath because she is trustworthy regarding 'iddah, but she is suspected of lying, so she is made to take an oath like the person with whom something is deposited. Explanation: The ruling is that the woman's statement with an oath is accepted because she is the only one who can know when 'iddah ends, but due to possibility of lying, she must swear, just like a depositary who claims to have returned or lost the deposit.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof. It states that when a woman claims her 'iddah has ended and her husband denies, her word is accepted with an oath. The reasoning is that she is the only person who possesses knowledge of her 'iddah, making her a trustworthy party (aminah) in this matter. However, because she may be dishonest, an oath is required to verify her claim. This is analogous to the case of a depositary (muda') who claims to have returned or destroyed the deposited item; his word is accepted with an oath. The proof is based on qiyas (analogy) to the depositary, where trustworthiness is combined with potential falsity, necessitating an oath. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that the woman's statement is accepted with an oath.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
قول (qawl): The statement or claim of the woman.
يمين (yamin): Oath sworn by the woman to support her claim.
أمين (amin): Trustworthy person; here the woman is considered amin concerning knowledge of her 'iddah.
مودع (muda'): Depositary; a person with whom something is kept in trust.

Mahr and 'Iddah in Remarriage During 'Iddah: The Shaykhayn vs Muhammad and Zufar
Intra-Hanafi dispute with analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shaykhayn's position (full mahr and new 'iddah) and explain the analogy of the usurper that supports it, while also distinguishing the views of Muhammad and Zufar.

Core Idea
First consummation's possession carries over to the second marriage, making divorce after consummation.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: a man divorces his wife with a final divorce (talaq ba'in), then remarries her during her 'iddah, and divorces her again before any new consummation. The key question is whether the first consummation counts as consummation for the second marriage, affecting mahr and 'iddah obligations. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue it does, while Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar disagree. Through careful analysis of their proofs, you'll understand how Hanafi jurists use possession (qabd) and analogical reasoning to resolve complex marital scenarios.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the sequence: talaq ba'in → remarriage during 'iddah → second divorce before consummation. This is the case in dispute.
2
Identify the three positions
List the views: Shaykhayn (full mahr + new 'iddah), Muhammad (half mahr + complete first 'iddah), Zufar (half mahr + no 'iddah).
3
Trace the Shaykhayn's proof
Understand their analogy: the woman is in the husband's possession (qabd) from the first intercourse. This possession substitutes for the possession normally required in the new marriage, so the second divorce is treated as after consummation.
The analogy: a usurper (ghasib) who buys the usurped property in his hand becomes a possessor by contract alone.
4
Analyze Muhammad's objection
Muhammad argues that the second divorce is before consummation (no new dukhul), so only half mahr and completion of the first 'iddah are due. He compares it to buying an umm walad and then freeing her.
Muhammad's view relies on the literal absence of new physical intimacy.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Contrast the practical consequences: full vs half mahr, and new 'iddah vs completion of old 'iddah vs no 'iddah. Determine which view is mufta bihi.
The Shaykhayn's view is relied upon in the school.

سے خرید لیا ، پھر اس کو آزاد کیا تو اس معتقہ باندی پر تین حیض واجب ہیں، دو حیض زوال نکاح کی وجہ سے اور ایک حیض آزاد ہونے کی وجہ
And if a man divorces his wife with a final divorce (bain), then marries her during her 'iddah, and divorces her before consummation, then upon him is full mahr and upon her is a complete new 'iddah. This is according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Upon him is half the mahr, and upon her is the completion of the first 'iddah, because this is a divorce before consummation, so it does not necessitate full mahr nor a new 'iddah. The completion of the first 'iddah is only required by the first divorce, but the situation of the second marriage did not appear; so when it is removed by the second divorce, the ruling of the first divorce becomes apparent, like if someone buys his umm walad and then frees her. Their (Shaykhayn's) proof is that she is in his actual possession due to the first intercourse, and its effect, the 'iddah, remains. So when he contracts a new marriage while she is in his possession, that possession stands in place of the possession required in this new marriage, like a usurper who buys the usurped property in his hand; he becomes a possessor by the mere contract. Thus it is clear that this divorce is after consummation. Zufar said: No 'iddah is required upon her at all, because the first 'iddah fell by the marriage and does not return, and the second was not obligatory. The answer is what we stated. ... [Urdu explanation and further detail]
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scenario where a man divorces his wife with a final divorce (bain), then remarries her during her 'iddah, and then divorces her again before consummation. The key point of contention is whether the first consummation (from the first marriage) counts as consummation for the second marriage. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn) hold that it does, because the woman remains in the husband's possession (qabd) due to the effect of the first intercourse, and this possession suffices for the second marriage. Therefore, the second divorce is considered after consummation, requiring full mahr and a new 'iddah. Imam Muhammad disagrees; he argues that the second divorce is before consummation since the new marriage has not been consummated. Thus, only half mahr is due, and the woman must complete the first 'iddah. Zufar goes further, stating that no 'iddah is required at all because the first 'iddah fell with the remarriage and the second never became obligatory. The Shaykhayn's view is considered the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, supported by analogy to a usurper buying usurped property. The text also includes a detailed analogy involving an umm walad to illustrate Muhammad's reasoning. Ultimately, the dispute hinges on the principle of whether the first consumption's possession transfers to the new marriage.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): A final divorce that ends the marriage irrevocably, requiring a new marriage contract for remarriage.
عدة (iddah): Waiting period observed by a woman after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
مهر (mahr): Mandatory payment from husband to wife at marriage; full or half depending on consummation.
دخول (dukhul): Consummation of marriage; physical intercourse that triggers certain legal effects.
قبض (qabd): Possession; here referring to the husband's possession of the wife through consummation.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes possession of another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what mahr and 'iddah are required when a man divorces his wife bain, remarries her in 'iddah, and divorces before consummation?
A) Half mahr and complete first 'iddah
B) Full mahr and a new 'iddah
C) Full mahr and no 'iddah
D) Half mahr and no 'iddah

Key Takeaway
The Shaykhayn's view that prior consummation carries over to a new marriage during 'iddah is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, requiring full mahr and a new 'iddah.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Mahr and Iddah After Bain Talaq and Remarriage
Explains ruling and four Hanafi positions on mahr and iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on mahr and iddah in the scenario of bain talaq followed by remarriage and divorce before consummation, and explain the key analogy (qiyas) used by the Shaykhayn.

Core Idea
Does first marriage's consummation count for second marriage? Three Hanafi imams differ on mahr and iddah.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the implications of remarrying a wife during her iddah after a bain divorce, then divorcing her before consummation. The core question is whether the first marriage's consummation 'transfers' to the second marriage, affecting both the mahr and the need for a new iddah. We will dissect the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Imam Muhammad, and Imam Zufar, focusing on their legal reasoning and analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed issues
Clearly define the factual scenario: bain talaq, remarriage during iddah, then divorce before consummation. The two disputed outcomes are: (1) full vs. half mahr, and (2) new iddah vs. continuation of first iddah vs. no iddah.
2
State each Hanafi position concisely
List the three views: Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf) = full mahr + new iddah; Imam Muhammad = half mahr + complete first iddah; Imam Zufar = half mahr + no iddah at all.
3
Identify the underlying principle (dabt)
The entire dispute hinges on whether consummation (dukhūl) in the first marriage counts as consummation for the second marriage. This is the key legal principle that differentiates the views.
4
Analyze the proofs and analogies
Examine Imam Muhammad's analogy of the slave-girl (umm walad) and the Shaykhayn's analogy of the usurper (ghāṣib). Understand how each analogy supports their respective positions on the transfer of consummation.
Focus on the 'possession' (qabḍah) argument in the ghāṣib analogy.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Determine the practical difference: under Shaykhayn, the wife begins a new iddah and receives full mahr; under Imam Muhammad, she continues her original iddah and gets half mahr; under Imam Zufar, she gets half mahr and no iddah at all (which would allow her to remarry immediately).

فرمالیا جائے۔
Chapter of Iddah. Translation: And if a man gave his wife a bain divorce, then during her iddah he married her and divorced her before consummation, then upon the husband is the full mahr and upon the woman a new iddah is obligatory. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad said: upon the husband is half the mahr and upon the woman completing the first iddah is obligatory, because this divorce is before consummation, so it will not necessitate full mahr nor a new iddah, and completing the first iddah is due to the first divorce, except that the effect of the second marriage did not appear. When the second marriage is removed by the second divorce, the ruling of the first divorce appears, like someone who bought his umm walad and then freed her. The proof of the Shaykhayn is that this woman is effectively in the possession of her husband due to the first marriage, and the effect of the first marriage i.e., the iddah still remains. So when he made a new marriage while she is in his possession, that possession stands in place of the possession required by the second marriage, like a usurper who purchases the usurped item that is in his possession, so the usurper buyer becomes possessed by virtue of the contract alone. So from this it became clear that this divorce is after consummation. Imam Zufar said: no iddah is obligatory upon this woman at all, because the first iddah was cancelled by the second marriage and will not return, and the second iddah did not become obligatory. The answer is what we have already explained. Explanation: The case is that if a man gave his wife a bain divorce, then during her iddah he remarried her, and before any intercourse or valid seclusion he divorced her, then there is disagreement among Hanafi scholars. According to the two shaykhs, due to the second marriage and divorce, full mahr is obligatory and a new iddah becomes obligatory. According to Imam Muhammad, half mahr and completing the first iddah is obligatory. According to Imam Zufar, half mahr and no iddah at all. This issue hinges on a principle: whether consummation in the first marriage counts as consummation in the second marriage. Imam Muhammad says: first consummation will not count as second. The two shaykhs say: it will count. Imam Muhammad's proof: The divorce after the second marriage is before intercourse and valid seclusion, and in case of divorce before intercourse and seclusion, full mahr is not obligatory nor is iddah. So half mahr, not full, and no new iddah, but completion of the first iddah is obligatory. Why is completion of first iddah obligatory? Because the first divorce made iddah obligatory, but the second marriage did not show its effect. When she was divorced without consummation, the second marriage became null, as if it never happened. So the effect of the first divorce appears and completing the first iddah becomes obligatory. An example: A person married another's slave-girl and had a child with her, then he bought her from her master, then freed her. Upon this freed slave-girl three menstrual periods are obligatory: two due to dissolution of marriage and one due to emancipation. Because when he bought her, the marriage became invalid, and two periods of iddah became obligatory. Her marriage to someone else is not valid until the iddah passes. But the ruling of iddah did not appear in her own right because the impediment of iddah, i.e., ownership of the right hand, existed. When he freed her, the impediment was removed, so the ruling of iddah appears. So two periods as iddah due to invalid marriage are obligatory, and mourning is necessary for them; the third period is due to emancipation, and mourning is not necessary. The proof of the two shaykhs: This woman is effectively in the possession of her husband due to the first marriage, and the effect of the first marriage i.e., iddah still remains. So when the husband renewed the marriage during the iddah while she is possessed due to first marriage, that possession stands in place of the possession required by the second marriage. So when he divorced her, it is as if he divorced her after consummation in the second marriage. Therefore, full mahr and a new iddah are obligatory. Its analogy: A usurper buys the usurped item from the owner while it is in his possession; then the usurper becomes possessed by the contract alone, no new possession needed. So it is established that the divorce after the second marriage is after consummation, not before. Imam Zufar's proof: The second marriage cancels the first iddah, so it does not return, because the rule is 'the cancelled does not return', and the second iddah is not obligatory because the divorce occurred before consummation. The answer to Imam Zufar's proof has been given earlier.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a specific scenario: a man gives his wife a bain (irrevocable) divorce, then remarries her during her iddah, and then divorces her again before any consummation or valid seclusion. The question is what mahr (dower) is due and whether a new iddah begins. The author first states the ruling of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): full mahr and a new iddah. Then presents Imam Muhammad's view: half mahr and continuation of the first iddah. Then Imam Zufar's view: half mahr and no iddah at all. The passage then explains that the dispute hinges on whether the first marriage's consummation counts as consummation for the second marriage. Imam Muhammad argues it does not, so the second divorce is before consummation, hence half mahr and no new iddah. The Shaykhayn argue that the first consummation carries over because the woman remains in the husband's possession (like a usurper purchasing a stolen item), so the second divorce is after consummation, hence full mahr and a new iddah. Imam Zufar says the second marriage cancels the first iddah permanently (since 'the cancelled does not return'), and no new iddah because of no consummation. The author rebuffs Imam Zufar's argument by reference to an earlier answer. An elaborate analogy with a slave-girl (umm walad) purchased and freed is provided to illustrate Imam Muhammad's reasoning about hidden effects of rulings. The passage is a detailed expansion of the matn and is typical of a sharh work like Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
عدت (iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death before remarriage, required to ensure no pregnancy
طلاق بائن (talāq bā’in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; husband cannot take back wife except with new contract
دخول (dukhūl): Sexual intercourse (actual consummation of marriage)
مہر (mahr): Dower; mandatory gift from husband to wife upon marriage
قبضہ (qabḍah): Possession; legal control over property or person

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Zufar, why is no iddah required after the second divorce in the given scenario?
A) The second marriage is invalid, so no iddah arises.
B) The first iddah was cancelled by the second marriage and cannot return, and the second divorce is before consummation.
C) The wife is not in the husband's possession, so no iddah.
D) The first iddah had already expired.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on mahr and iddah after bain talaq and remarriage hinges on whether consummation from the first marriage transfers to the second; the Shaykhayn's view (full mahr, new iddah) is the mufta bihi.

Talaq — Divorce

Iddah for Dhimmi and Harbi Women: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iddah for dhimmi and harbi women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on iddah for dhimmi and harbi women, identify the proofs for each, and apply the preferred ruling (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: no iddah unless pregnant; Sahibayn: iddah required. The preferred view is Abu Hanifa's.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a dhimmi woman (protected non-Muslim) must observe iddah after divorce by her dhimmi husband, and whether a harbiyah woman (enemy non-Muslim) who converts to Islam and enters dar al-Islam must observe iddah. The two schools of thought within the Hanafi madhhab—Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad)—reach opposite conclusions, each with a distinct proof structure. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate ikhtilaf within the school and apply the mufta bihi (operative ruling).

Guided Path
1
Understand the two cases
The passage addresses two scenarios: (1) a dhimmi divorces a dhimmi wife; (2) a harbiyah woman converts to Islam and enters dar al-Islam.
2
Identify the positions
State Abu Hanifa's ruling (no iddah unless pregnant) and the Sahibayn's ruling (iddah required).
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
He cites Quran 60:10 ('There is no sin upon you that you marry them') and the principle that the harbi is like an inanimate object, lacking human rights for iddah. Exception: pregnancy because lineage is established.
4
Analyze Sahibayn's proof
They use qiyas: if separation occurred by another cause (e.g., apostasy), iddah would be required, so here the difference of religion should also necessitate iddah.
5
Determine the tarjih
The author states 'والاول اصح' (the first is more correct), indicating preference for Abu Hanifa's view, which becomes the mufta bihi.

واذا طلق الذمى الذمية فلاعدة عليها وكذا اذا خرجت الحربية الينا مسلمة فان تزوجت جازالا ان تكون حاملا وهذا كله عندابي حنيفة وقالا عليها وعلى الذمية العدة اما الذمية فالاختلاف فيها نظير الاختلاف في نكاحهم محارمهم وقدبيناه في كتاب النكاح وقول ابى حنيفة فيما اذا كان معتقدهم انه لاعدة عليها و اما المهاجرة فوجه قولهما ان الفرقة لو وقعت بسبب اخر وجبت العدة فكذا بسبب التباين بخلاف ما اذا هاجر الرجل وتركها لعدم التبليغ وله قوله تعالى لا جناح عليكم ان تنكحوهن ولان العدة حيث وجبت كان فيها حق بنى آدم والحربي ملحق بالجماد حتى كان محلا للتملك الا ان تكون حاملا لان في بطنها ولداثابت النسب و عن ابي حنيفة انه يجوز نكاحها ولا يطاها كالحبلى من الزناء والاول اصح ترجمہ اور اگر زمی نے زمیہ کو طلاق دی تو زمیہ عورت پر عدت نہیں ہے اور ایسے ہی اگر در بسیہ عورت مسلمان ہو کر ہمارے ملک میں ) نکل آئی ( تو اس پر عدت نہیں ) چنانچہ اگر اس نے نکاح کیا تو جائز ہے مگر یہ کہ حاملہ ہواور یہ سب امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اس عورت پر اور زمیہ پر عدت واجب ہے۔ بہر حال زمیہ تو اس کے بارے میں اختلاف کی نظیر ہے جو ذمیوں کا اپنی محارم کے ساتھ نکاح کرنے کے بارے میں ہے اور ہم اس کو اپنی
And if a dhimmi divorces a dhimmi woman, there is no iddah upon her. Similarly, if a harbiyah woman comes out to us as a Muslim, then if she marries it is permissible, unless she is pregnant. All this is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Sahibayn) said: both this woman and the dhimmi woman are required to observe iddah. As for the dhimmi woman, the disagreement is similar to the disagreement regarding their marriage with their mahram, which we explained in the Book of Nikah. Abu Hanifa's statement is when their belief is that there is no iddah upon her. As for the immigrant woman, the basis of their (Sahibayn's) statement is that if separation occurred by another cause, iddah would be obligatory, so here due to difference of religion as well. Contrary to the case when a man emigrates and leaves her, due to lack of communication. And he (Abu Hanifa) has the verse: 'There is no sin upon you that you marry them'. And because iddah where it is obligatory includes a right of humans, and the harbi is like an inanimate object, such that she is a subject for ownership, unless she is pregnant, because in her womb there is a child with established lineage. And from Abu Hanifa that it is permissible to marry her but not to have intercourse with her, like a pregnant woman from adultery. And the first is more correct. Translation: And if a dhimmi divorced a dhimmi woman, then there is no iddah upon the dhimmi woman. Similarly, if a harbiyah woman came to us as a Muslim, then there is no iddah upon her. If she marries, it is permissible, unless she is pregnant. All this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn said: upon this woman and upon the dhimmi woman, iddah is obligatory. Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) regarding the iddah of a dhimmi woman (protected non-Muslim citizen) divorced by a dhimmi husband, and a harbiyah woman (enemy non-Muslim) who converts to Islam and enters dar al-Islam. According to Abu Hanifa, neither woman must observe iddah unless she is pregnant. The Sahibayn require iddah in both cases. Abu Hanifa's proof includes the Quranic verse (Q. 60:10) indicating no sin in marrying them, and the reasoning that iddah involves human rights, but a harbi is like an inanimate object (can be owned), so no iddah. The exception is pregnancy because the child's lineage is established. Additionally, Abu Hanifa rules that such a woman may be married but intercourse must wait until after delivery, similar to the rule for a pregnant woman from adultery. The Sahibayn analogize to other separations where iddah is required. The author notes that the dispute is analogous to the issue of dhimmi marriage to mahram (forbidden relatives), and then marks 'the first is more correct' (al-awwal asahh), indicating preference for Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmi): Non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with protection (dhimma)
حربی (harbi): Non-Muslim from enemy territory not under Muslim protection
عدت (iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death before remarriage
ساقط لا یعود (al-sāqit lā ya‘ūd): That which falls does not return; a legal maxim

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She must observe a full iddah of three menstrual cycles
B) She may remarry immediately regardless of pregnancy
C) She may remarry immediately unless she is pregnant
D) She must wait until she completes one menstrual cycle

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that no iddah is required for a dhimmi woman after divorce or a harbi convert, unless she is pregnant—a ruling rooted in Quranic permission and the harbi's unique legal status.
